Chapter 3
Requirement Analysis
3.1 Introduction to Requirement Analysis

This chapter presents a structured and formal specification of system requirements for the proposed Autonomous Delivery Drone project. The purpose of this chapter is to clearly define what the system shall accomplish before proceeding to the system design and implementation phases. The requirements documented in this chapter serve as a contractual foundation for implementation, validation, and system testing.

The requirement analysis includes requirement elicitation techniques, identification of stakeholders, use case modeling, functional requirements, non-functional requirements, and external interface requirements. By bridging the gap between the high-level project scope and the low-level microservice architecture (combining ROS 2, FastAPI, React.js, and OpenCV), this chapter establishes a definitive baseline. All requirements are written in a measurable and testable format using standard requirement engineering principles.
3.2 Requirement Elicitation Techniques

This section describes the techniques used to gather and refine system requirements. Requirement elicitation ensures absolute alignment between stakeholder expectations and the system's technical capabilities. Requirements for this autonomous UAV project were rigorously identified using the following techniques:

    Stakeholder Interviews & Consultations: Frequent discussions with the project supervisor (Dr. Rab Nawaz Bashir) to capture academic expectations, safety constraints, and evaluation metrics.

    Literature Review & Architectural Analysis: An extensive review of similar commercial systems (e.g., Amazon Prime Air, Zipline), as well as academic research contributions focusing on Potential Field Navigation, Velocity Blending, and Lidar-based spatial reflexes.

    Analysis of Existing Solutions: Examination of existing ground control software (like QGroundControl) to identify functional gaps, specifically the cognitive overload they present to non-technical logistics operators.

    Simulation & Feasibility Studies: Conducting intensive Software-In-The-Loop (SITL) testing within the Gazebo Harmonic physics engine to extract empirical data regarding flight dynamics, Extended Kalman Filter (EKF) failures, and sensor pitch-blindness during high-speed evasions.

    Observation of Workflow Processes: Mapping out the traditional last-mile delivery workflow to design a streamlined, click-to-dispatch digital pipeline.

3.3 User Classes and Characteristics

The system is designed to cater to distinct user groups, each interacting with different architectural layers of the project.

    Logistics Operator / Dispatcher: This is the primary end-user interacting with the React.js Ground Control Station (GCS). They possess minimal to zero technical knowledge regarding aerodynamics, PID tuning, or MAVLink protocols. Their primary characteristics include a need for high usability, clear visual feedback, and automated safety triggers. They focus purely on defining drop-off coordinates and monitoring the delivery status.

    System Administrator / Fleet Manager: A technical user responsible for the backend maintenance of the UAV fleet. This user requires access to the system’s backend modules to review post-mission analytics, download CSV flight logs, monitor Lidar evasion occurrences, and calibrate the ROS 2 and OpenCV microservices.

3.4 System Overview

The Autonomous Delivery Drone is a modular, microservice-driven cyber-physical system designed to automate last-mile logistics in dynamic environments. Traditional drone delivery systems rely heavily on static GPS waypoints and manual piloting. This system radically departs from that methodology by implementing Continuous Velocity Field Navigation.

Governed by an onboard Raspberry Pi companion computer running a FastAPI backend, the drone is fed a continuous stream of velocity vectors. Concurrently, a Robot Operating System (ROS 2) middleware node actively processes raw 2D Lidar data. When a physical obstruction enters the flight path, the ROS 2 node calculates an evasion trajectory, seamlessly blending lateral velocity into the drone's movement to curve around the obstacle without halting or compromising aerodynamic stability. Upon reaching the target destination, an OpenCV computer vision pipeline assumes localized control, actively scanning for ArUco landing markers to perform a mathematically precise, wind-corrected touchdown.
3.5 Use Case Model

The Use Case model provides a high-level visual and textual representation of how the external actors interact with the system boundaries to achieve specific logistical goals.
3.5.1 Use Case Diagram

(Note: Insert your UML Use Case Diagram here. The diagram should show the Logistics Operator interacting with "Dispatch Drone" and "Monitor Telemetry", while system actors like ROS 2 and OpenCV interact with "Execute Evasion" and "Precision Landing".)
3.5.2 Use Case Descriptions
3.5.2.1 Module 1 & 2: Web GCS & Core Flight Control

Use Case UC-1.1: Autonomous Drone Dispatch

    Actors: Logistics Operator (Primary)

    Description: The operator utilizes the web-based map interface to establish a destination and authorizes the UAV to begin its autonomous delivery sequence.

    Preconditions: The drone is physically armed, the battery state of charge is > 80%, GPS lock is confirmed (3D Fix), and WebSocket telemetry streams are active.

    Postconditions: The drone autonomously ascends to the target cruising altitude (10m) and initiates continuous velocity navigation toward the specified coordinates.

    Normal Flow: 1. The operator interfaces with the interactive Leaflet map to drop a destination pin.
    2. The operator selects the "Confirm Mission & Dispatch" command.
    3. The React.js frontend transmits an asynchronous POST request containing the coordinates to the FastAPI backend.
    4. The backend computes the dynamic compass bearing and initiates the takeoff sequence via MAVSDK.
    5. The dashboard UI state transitions to "Navigating," visualizing the drone's real-time trajectory.

    Exceptions: If the drone lacks a stable GPS lock, the backend rejects the command, the dashboard displays a critical warning, and the drone remains grounded.

3.5.2.2 Module 3: Spatial Reflex System

Use Case UC-2.1: Dynamic Lidar Obstacle Evasion

    Actors: System Actor (ROS 2 Node / FastAPI Backend)

    Description: The system automatically detects an unmapped physical obstruction and actively adjusts its velocity vectors to slide around the object without manual intervention.

    Preconditions: The drone is in active autonomous transit. The ROS 2 Lidar bridge and Avoider nodes are broadcasting securely over local UDP port 5006.

    Normal Flow:

        The 2D Lidar sensor registers a proximity breach within the predefined 7.0-meter danger threshold.

        The ROS 2 node computes the optimal escape route and locks the evasion direction (e.g., DODGE_RIGHT) into its local memory to prevent oscillation.

        The FastAPI flight controller intercepts the UDP broadcast.

        The drone executes a gentle aerodynamic brake and hovers for 1.5 seconds to physically level its sensors (preventing Lidar pitch-blindness).

        The drone executes a lateral velocity slide until the ROS 2 node confirms a "CLEAR" state for 2 continuous seconds.

        The drone bypasses the obstacle edge, recalculates its bearing, and resumes the primary mission.

3.5.2.3 Module 4: Precision Vision System

Use Case UC-3.1: Vision-Assisted Touchdown

    Actors: System Actor (OpenCV Node)

    Description: Upon arriving at the geographic vicinity of the destination, the system utilizes computer vision to guarantee millimeter-accurate payload delivery on a designated landing pad.

    Preconditions: The drone is hovering over the target GPS coordinates. The landing zone is adequately lit, and the specified ArUco marker is present.

    Normal Flow:

        The OpenCV script parses the downward-facing camera feed.

        The script identifies the specific ArUco marker dictionary and extracts its center point.

        The script calculates the X and Y pixel error relative to the camera frame's center.

        A Proportional-Integral-Derivative (PID) controller translates the pixel error into physical lateral velocity adjustments (m/s).

        The drone descends while continuously correcting its alignment against environmental wind drift.

        Upon reaching an altitude of < 0.3m, the drone executes a physical landing, disarms its motors, and actuates the payload servo.

3.5.3 Event-Response Table

The following table maps critical system events to their corresponding automated responses, ensuring system reliability under varying operational conditions.
Event	System State	Response
Operator confirms map coordinates and clicks "Dispatch".	Standby / Idle	Backend verifies GPS, calculates bearing, arms motors, initiates takeoff to 10m, and begins velocity field navigation.
Drone arrives within 0.000015 degrees of target GPS.	Navigating	Navigation halts. Drone transitions to a stable hover and hands control over to the OpenCV Precision Landing loop.
Lidar node detects solid object < 7.0 meters ahead.	Navigating	ROS 2 node locks evasion direction. Flight controller brakes, hovers to level sensors, and executes lateral velocity slide.
Lidar node reads "CLEAR" for 2 continuous seconds.	Evading Obstacle	Drone pushes forward to bypass the obstacle edge, recalculates the destination bearing, and resumes the trajectory.
ArUco marker not found after 45 seconds of hovering.	Landing Sequence	Vision Failsafe activates. Landing is aborted, the drone ascends to a safe altitude, and triggers Return-to-Launch (RTL).
Battery voltage drops below predefined Critical Threshold.	Any Active State	Hardware failsafe triggers; the drone performs an immediate emergency landing at its current geographic location to prevent battery failure mid-air.
3.6 Functional Requirements

Functional requirements define the core operational capabilities that the system must possess to satisfy the project's objectives. They are categorized by the system's modular architecture.
3.6.1 Module 1: Core Flight Control (FastAPI Backend)

    FR-1.1 Continuous Velocity Navigation: The system shall utilize offboard VelocityNedYaw commands to continuously stream movement vectors to the drone. It shall strictly avoid static goto_location waypoints during transit to maintain EKF stability during evasive maneuvers.

    FR-1.2 Dynamic Bearing Calculation: The system shall mathematically compute the geographic compass bearing between its current telemetry location and the destination, ensuring the drone's nose (and the rigid Lidar sensor) always faces the direction of travel.

3.6.2 Module 2: Web Ground Control Station (Frontend)

    FR-2.1 Interactive Mission Planning: The React.js frontend shall provide an interactive map interface allowing the operator to click and establish precise latitudinal and longitudinal coordinates for the delivery destination.

    FR-2.2 Emergency Override Capability: The UI shall provide a highly accessible, zero-latency "Emergency Land" and "Return to Home (RTL)" button that immediately interrupts any active autonomous script.

3.6.3 Module 3: Spatial Reflex System (ROS 2 Obstacle Avoidance)

    FR-3.1 Evasion Direction Locking: The ROS 2 node shall evaluate the left and right Lidar proximity arrays upon detecting an obstacle and lock the optimal evasion direction (DODGE_LEFT or DODGE_RIGHT) into memory, preventing erratic direction flipping.

    FR-3.2 Hover and Verify Debounce: To resolve Lidar pitch-blindness caused by aerodynamic braking, the flight logic shall command a 1.5-second stable hover to physically level the drone's pitch before trusting the "CLEAR" signal from the ROS 2 node.

3.6.4 Module 4: Precision Vision System (OpenCV)

    FR-4.1 Visual Error Calculation: The system shall process downward-facing camera frames to identify ArUco markers and calculate the instantaneous X and Y pixel deviation from the center of the frame.

    FR-4.2 Proportional Descent Control: The system shall apply a Proportional gain (K_p) to translate visual pixel errors into physical lateral velocities, ensuring the drone micro-corrects its position while descending at 0.4 m/s.

3.6.5 Module 5: Payload Actuation Controller

    FR-5.1 Synchronized Payload Release: The system shall actuate a PWM-controlled servo motor to release the delivery package only after the flight controller confirms the motors have been successfully disarmed post-landing.

3.6.6 Module 6: Communication Bridge & Safety Monitor

    FR-6.1 Live Telemetry Streaming: The backend shall maintain a persistent WebSocket connection to push real-time telemetry (Altitude, Battery, GPS, Mode, Lidar State) to the React.js dashboard at a minimum frequency of 5Hz.

3.6.7 Module 7: Mission Analytics & Logging

    FR-7.1 Flight Data Preservation: The system shall log crucial mission parameters (Flight ID, Dispatch Time, Duration, Battery Consumption, and Total Evasion Events) into a local database for post-flight administrative review.

3.7 Non-Functional Requirements

Non-functional requirements specify the quality attributes, performance goals, and constraints of the system.
3.7.1 Performance Requirements

    Low Latency Sensor Processing: The ROS 2 Lidar bridge and Python obstacle avoidance logic must process 360-degree proximity arrays and broadcast UDP velocity setpoints at a minimum frequency of 10Hz to ensure the drone reacts safely to obstacles while traveling at cruising speeds.

    Vision Processing Speed: The OpenCV node must process video frames rapidly enough to provide real-time coordinate errors, preventing over-correction and pendulum oscillations during the descent phase.

3.7.2 Security Requirements

    Microservice Isolation: The UDP ports utilized for the Vision System (Port 5005) and Lidar System (Port 5006) must be strictly bound locally (127.0.0.1) on the companion computer to prevent external network interference, spoofing, or hijacking of the drone's spatial reflex commands.

    API Command Authentication: The FastAPI backend executing the physical MAVSDK commands must be configured to accept REST API dispatch triggers exclusively from the authenticated Ground Control Station environment.

3.7.3 Reliability Requirements

    Aerodynamic Stability Preservation: The system must restrict sudden raw velocity injections (e.g., slamming the brakes instantly from 10 m/s to -5 m/s) to prevent the flight controller's Extended Kalman Filter (EKF) from experiencing mathematical divergence, which would cause an uncontrollable crash.

    Failsafe Redundancy: If the companion computer loses connection to the Ground Control Station for more than 10 seconds, the system must automatically fall back to the hardware-level Return to Launch (RTL) protocol.

3.7.4 Usability Requirements

    Cognitive Load Reduction: The React.js Ground Control Station must abstract complex MAVLink configurations (e.g., PID tuning, yaw rates, calibration) entirely away from the logistics operator, presenting only intuitive map visuals, clear status text, and simple execution buttons.

3.7.5 Scalability Requirements

    Modular Node Architecture: Because the Vision, Lidar, and Web control systems are segregated into independent microservices communicating via UDP and WebSockets, the architecture must allow for isolated hardware upgrades (e.g., upgrading from a 2D Lidar to a 3D Depth Camera) without requiring a rewrite of the core flight logic.

3.7.6 Maintainability Requirements

    Codebase Documentation: All Python, ROS 2, and React codebases must maintain rigorous, PEP-8 compliant documentation regarding coordinate mathematics, matrix rotations, and hardware port mappings to facilitate seamless academic handover or future physical hardware integration.

3.8 External Interface Requirements
3.8.1 User Interface Requirements

The primary interface is a React.js single-page application functioning as the Ground Control Station (GCS). It utilizes the react-leaflet library to render a dynamic, high-resolution map interface. The UI must feature a responsive sidebar for live telemetry logging and highly visible, color-coded action buttons (Green for Dispatch, Red for Emergency Stop).
3.8.2 Hardware Interface Requirements

    Flight Controller: A Pixhawk (or equivalent MAVLink-compatible flight management unit) running PX4 Autopilot firmware to handle low-level motor mixing and stabilization.

    Companion Computer: A Raspberry Pi 4/5 utilized to execute the high-level Python logic, ROS 2 nodes, and OpenCV processing locally on the drone.

    Sensors: An RPLidar A1 (or equivalent 2D Lidar) connected via serial/USB for spatial mapping, alongside a standard downward-facing USB webcam for computer vision.

3.8.3 Software Interface Requirements

    ROS 2 Humble: Utilized as the primary robotic middleware for real-time sensor node execution and bridging.

    MAVSDK-Python: The primary asynchronous library interfacing the high-level Python logic with the PX4 flight stack via the MAVLink protocol.

    Gazebo Harmonic: The 3D physics engine used for exhaustive Software-In-The-Loop (SITL) simulations.

3.8.4 Communication Interface Requirements

    MAVLink Protocol: The FastAPI application communicates with the PX4 SITL or physical hardware over udp://:14540.

    Internal UDP Microservices: The Vision Bridge broadcasts vector data exclusively on 127.0.0.1:5005, and the ROS 2 Lidar Bridge broadcasts on 127.0.0.1:5006.

    WebSockets: The backend pushes live JSON telemetry payloads to the React frontend via standard WebSockets over local Wi-Fi.

3.9 Assumptions and Dependencies

    Visual Line of Sight (VLOS) Operation: It is assumed the drone operates entirely within the range of a robust local Wi-Fi network to maintain its connection with the Web GCS.

    Failsafe Override Configuration: It is assumed that specific PX4 parameters (such as COM_LOW_BAT_ACT) are correctly configured prior to launch to prevent built-in battery failsafes from prematurely hijacking the Python execution loops.

    Environmental Lighting: The Precision Vision System heavily depends on the assumption that operations occur in daylight or well-lit environments, as the camera cannot reliably process ArUco markers in darkness.

3.10 Requirement Traceability Matrix

The traceability matrix establishes a direct link between the high-level Use Cases and the specific Functional Requirements designed to fulfill them.
Use Case ID	Functional Requirement ID	Description of Mapped Requirement
UC-1.1	FR-1.1, FR-2.1, FR-6.1	Operator dispatches drone; backend streams continuous velocity and live telemetry over WebSockets.

UC-2.1	FR-3.1, FR-1.1	ROS 2 node locks the evasion direction and broadcasts velocity blending overrides.

3.5.2 Use Case Descriptions

3.5.2.1 Module 1 & 2: Web GCS & Core Flight Control

Use Case UC-1.1: Autonomous Drone Dispatch


Actors: Logistics Operator (Primary)

Description: The operator utilizes the web-based map interface to establish a destination and authorizes the UAV to begin its autonomous delivery sequence.

Preconditions: The drone is physically armed, the battery state of charge is > 80%, GPS lock is confirmed (3D Fix), and WebSocket telemetry streams are active.

Postconditions: The drone autonomously ascends to the target cruising altitude (10m) and initiates continuous velocity navigation toward the specified coordinates.

Normal Flow: 1. The operator interfaces with the interactive Leaflet map to drop a destination pin.

2. The operator selects the "Confirm Mission & Dispatch" command.

3. The React.js frontend transmits an asynchronous POST request containing the coordinates to the FastAPI backend.

4. The backend computes the dynamic compass bearing and initiates the takeoff sequence via MAVSDK.

5. The dashboard UI state transitions to "Navigating," visualizing the drone's real-time trajectory.

Exceptions: If the drone lacks a stable GPS lock, the backend rejects the command, the dashboard displays a critical warning, and the drone remains grounded.

3.5.2.2 Module 3: Spatial Reflex System

Use Case UC-2.1: Dynamic Lidar Obstacle Evasion


Actors: System Actor (ROS 2 Node / FastAPI Backend)

Description: The system automatically detects an unmapped physical obstruction and actively adjusts its velocity vectors to slide around the object without manual intervention.

Preconditions: The drone is in active autonomous transit. The ROS 2 Lidar bridge and Avoider nodes are broadcasting securely over local UDP port 5006.

Normal Flow:

The 2D Lidar sensor registers a proximity breach within the predefined 7.0-meter danger threshold.

The ROS 2 node computes the optimal escape route and locks the evasion direction (e.g., DODGE_RIGHT) into its local memory to prevent oscillation.

The FastAPI flight controller intercepts the UDP broadcast.

The drone executes a gentle aerodynamic brake and hovers for 1.5 seconds to physically level its sensors (preventing Lidar pitch-blindness).

The drone executes a lateral velocity slide until the ROS 2 node confirms a "CLEAR" state for 2 continuous seconds.

The drone bypasses the obstacle edge, recalculates its bearing, and resumes the primary mission.

3.5.2.3 Module 4: Precision Vision System

Use Case UC-3.1: Vision-Assisted Touchdown


Actors: System Actor (OpenCV Node)

Description: Upon arriving at the geographic vicinity of the destination, the system utilizes computer vision to guarantee millimeter-accurate payload delivery on a designated landing pad.

Preconditions: The drone is hovering over the target GPS coordinates. The landing zone is adequately lit, and the specified ArUco marker is present.

Normal Flow:

The OpenCV script parses the downward-facing camera feed.

The script identifies the specific ArUco marker dictionary and extracts its center point.

The script calculates the X and Y pixel error relative to the camera frame's center.

A Proportional-Integral-Derivative (PID) controller translates the pixel error into physical lateral velocity adjustments (m/s).

The drone descends while continuously correcting its alignment against environmental wind drift.

Upon reaching an altitude of < 0.3m, the drone executes a physical landing, disarms its motors, and actuates the payload servo.

Modules


Module 1: Drone Flight Control (FastAPI Backend)

This module runs on the onboard computer and utilizes the MAVSDK library. It manages the core flight state, utilizing continuous offboard velocity control to translate trajectory calculations and evasion vectors into low-level MAVLink messages that the flight controller understands.


Module 2: Web Ground Control Station (React.js Frontend)

This module is the user interface developed in React.js. It features an interactive map (using Leaflet) where the user can set destination markers, dispatch the drone, and view the live position, remaining battery, and system status without needing technical flight knowledge.


Module 3: Spatial Reflex System (ROS 2 Obstacle Avoidance)

A dedicated ROS 2 middleware node that subscribes to raw 2D Lidar sensor data. It processes proximity arrays in real-time and broadcasts dynamic evasion vectors (e.g., DODGE_LEFT, DODGE_RIGHT) to the flight controller to seamlessly curve the drone around sudden physical obstacles.


Module 4: Precision Vision System (OpenCV)

A Computer Vision script that processes a downward-facing camera feed. Upon arriving at the GPS coordinates, it identifies specific ArUco markers, calculates the X/Y pixel error, and guides the drone into a mathematically precise touchdown, overcoming standard GPS drift.


Module 5: Communication Bridge & Safety Monitor

This module manages asynchronous data flow using WebSockets for the frontend UI and UDP sockets for internal microservices. It constantly monitors system health and triggers automated fail-safes (e.g., hover, brake, or Return-to-Launch) if sensors go blind, batteries hit critical thresholds, or connections drop.


Module 6: Payload Actuation Controller

This module manages the physical delivery mechanism (servo motor). It communicates with the Precision Vision System and coordinates the package release latch, ensuring the payload is only dropped once stable touchdown or exact alignment is verified.


Module 7: Mission Analytics & Logging Engine

This module captures real-time telemetry data, Lidar evasion events, flight durations, and battery consumption. It stores this data in a local database to generate post-flight reports and downloadable analytics (CSV) for the administrative dashboard.

It clearly defines the system boundaries and captures the functional behavior of the Autonomous Delivery Drone from a user and operational perspective. 3.5.2 Use Case Descriptions

This section provides detailed tabular specifications of the major use cases categorized directly by the system's seven architectural modules. These use cases form the comprehensive basis for deriving detailed functional requirements and test cases.
3.5.2.1 Module 1: Drone Flight Control (FastAPI Backend)

This module acts as the central brain of the physical drone, translating high-level directives into low-level MAVSDK commands.

Table 3.2: UC-1.1: Execute Continuous Velocity Navigation
Field	Description
Use Case ID	UC-1.1
Use Case Name	Execute Continuous Velocity Navigation
Actors	FastAPI Flight Controller (System)
Description	This use case defines how the backend manages the drone's transit state by sending continuous 10Hz offboard velocity vectors, rather than using static GPS waypoints, to maintain aerodynamic stability.
Trigger	The drone successfully reaches its 10-meter takeoff altitude after dispatch.
Preconditions	Drone is airborne, MAVSDK offboard mode is active, and target GPS coordinates are registered in the state manager.
Postconditions	The drone arrives at the 0.000015-degree threshold of the target destination.
Normal Flow	

1. The flight controller continuously retrieves the drone's current telemetry via MAVSDK.

2. The system calculates the dynamic geographic bearing to the target destination.

3. The backend generates a continuous forward velocity vector (e.g., 4.0 m/s).

4. A rotation matrix converts the forward vector into North and East velocities (VelocityNedYaw).

5. The velocity commands are pushed to the Pixhawk flight controller at 10Hz.

6. The system checks the remaining distance to the target on every loop iteration.

7. Upon reaching the threshold, the system halts forward velocity and transitions to a hover state.
Alternative Flow	If the target is updated mid-flight by the operator, the system instantly recalculates the bearing matrix and seamlessly shifts the velocity vector without halting the drone.
3.5.2.2 Module 2: Web Ground Control Station (React.js Frontend)

This module is the primary human-machine interface.

Table 3.3: UC-2.1: Mission Planning and Drone Dispatch
Field	Description
Use Case ID	UC-2.1
Use Case Name	Mission Planning and Drone Dispatch
Actors	Logistics Operator
Description	Describes how a non-technical logistics operator interacts with the web dashboard to define a drop-off zone and authorize the autonomous flight.
Trigger	The operator clicks the "Confirm Mission & Dispatch" button on the UI.
Preconditions	The user is authenticated, the React dashboard has an active WebSocket connection to the drone, and a valid map pin is placed.
Postconditions	A dispatch command is securely transmitted to the FastAPI backend, and the UI shifts to active monitoring mode.
Normal Flow	

1. Operator logs into the React.js GCS.

2. Operator navigates the interactive Leaflet map and clicks to drop a red destination pin.

3. The UI validates the coordinates against the maximum VLOS range (150 meters).

4. Operator presses the Dispatch button.

5. The frontend packages the coordinates into a JSON payload and issues an asynchronous POST request to the backend.

6. The backend acknowledges receipt.

7. The UI sidebar updates from "Standby" to "Navigating" and begins rendering live telemetry data.
Alternative Flow	If the selected destination exceeds the 150-meter geofence limit, the system rejects the pin placement, displays a red boundary warning, and disables the dispatch button.
3.5.2.3 Module 3: Spatial Reflex System (ROS 2 Obstacle Avoidance)

This module handles real-time physical survivability in unpredictable airspace.

Table 3.4: UC-3.1: Dynamic Lidar-Based Obstacle Evasion
Field	Description
Use Case ID	UC-3.1
Use Case Name	Dynamic Lidar-Based Obstacle Evasion
Actors	ROS 2 Avoider Node (System)
Description	Details how the drone detects an unmapped physical object and utilizes velocity blending to smoothly curve around the obstacle without triggering an Extended Kalman Filter (EKF) failure.
Trigger	The 2D Lidar proximity array detects a solid object breaching the 7.0-meter safety threshold.
Preconditions	The drone is navigating via offboard velocity control. The ros_gz_bridge and Avoider Node are actively subscribing to /lidar/scan.
Postconditions	The obstacle is bypassed, and the drone resumes its original heading toward the destination.
Normal Flow	

1. The ROS 2 node scans the Lidar array and detects an obstruction at < 7.0m.

2. The node evaluates left and right clearance and locks the optimal direction (e.g., DODGE_RIGHT) into state memory.

3. The decision is broadcasted via local UDP.

4. The FastAPI backend receives the signal and commands a gentle aerodynamic brake (-2.5 m/s) and a 1.5-second hover to level the drone's pitch.

5. The backend begins applying a blended lateral velocity (2.5 m/s) while maintaining a slight reverse thrust.

6. The ROS 2 node monitors the Lidar until 2 continuous seconds of "CLEAR" space is detected.

7. The backend clears the evasion state, pushes the drone forward to bypass the edge, and recalculates the bearing to the destination.
Alternative Flow	If both left and right directions are equally blocked (e.g., a massive wide wall), the system defaults to a safe vertical ascension protocol until clearance is achieved.
3.5.2.4 Module 4: Precision Vision System (OpenCV)

This module handles the highly delicate touchdown sequence.

Table 3.5: UC-4.1: ArUco Marker Alignment and Touchdown
Field	Description
Use Case ID	UC-4.1
Use Case Name	ArUco Marker Alignment and Touchdown
Actors	OpenCV Vision Node (System)
Description	Once the drone arrives at the GPS destination, this use case describes the computer vision process used to eliminate GPS drift and land exactly on a printed delivery pad.
Trigger	The drone completes the transit phase and enters the destination hover state.
Preconditions	The downward-facing camera is active, OpenCV UDP broadcasting is online, and ambient lighting is sufficient.
Postconditions	The drone is physically landed on the ground, centered on the marker, with motors disarmed.
Normal Flow	

1. The flight controller commands a stable hover at 10m.

2. The OpenCV script processes incoming video frames and detects ArUco Marker ID 0.

3. The script extracts the marker's center coordinates and calculates the X/Y pixel error relative to the camera frame center.

4. The pixel error is transmitted over UDP.

5. The FastAPI backend applies a Proportional (K_p) controller to translate visual error into lateral micro-adjustments.

6. The drone steadily descends at 0.4 m/s while actively fighting wind drift to stay centered.

7. Upon reaching < 0.3m altitude, the backend triggers the automated touchdown and motor disarm sequence.
Alternative Flow	If the ArUco marker is completely obscured or undetectable upon arrival, the drone will hold its hover for a maximum of 45 seconds. After this timeout, the system aborts the landing, gains safe altitude, and initiates a Return-to-Launch (RTL).
3.5.2.5 Module 5: Communication Bridge & Safety Monitor

This module is the silent guardian of the system, monitoring internal health.

Table 3.6: UC-5.1: Automated Failsafe Activation
Field	Description
Use Case ID	UC-5.1
Use Case Name	Automated Failsafe Activation
Actors	Safety Monitor (System)
Description	Monitors hardware voltages and network health, automatically taking control away from the mission planner if conditions become lethal to the drone.
Trigger	Battery drops below 15%, or WebSocket/telemetry heartbeat is lost for 10 seconds.
Preconditions	The drone is airborne in any state.
Postconditions	The drone is safely returned to its launch point or landed immediately.
Normal Flow	

1. The safety daemon polls battery voltage and network heartbeat at 5Hz.

2. The daemon detects a critical voltage drop (e.g., 14.5%).

3. The Safety Monitor instantly interrupts the active async tasks in the FastAPI backend.

4. An "EMERGENCY" status is pushed to the Web UI.

5. The flight controller overrides the current velocity field with an automated RTL (Return to Launch) command.

6. The drone ascends to a safe clearance altitude and flies back to the original home coordinates.
Alternative Flow	If the battery is at critical "Level 2" (e.g., < 5%), the system skips the RTL sequence and commands a direct, immediate vertical landing to prevent mid-air power failure.
3.5.2.6 Module 6: Payload Actuation Controller

This module controls the physical delivery mechanics.

Table 3.7: UC-6.1: Synchronized Payload Release
Field	Description
Use Case ID	UC-6.1
Use Case Name	Synchronized Payload Release
Actors	Actuation Controller (System)
Description	Ensures the package is dropped only when aerodynamic conditions are perfectly safe, avoiding damage to the payload or drone.
Trigger	The Pixhawk flight controller confirms a successful "Motors Disarmed" state post-landing.
Preconditions	The Precision Vision System has successfully landed the drone, and the payload latch is currently engaged.
Postconditions	The package is detached, and the drone prepares for the return flight.
Normal Flow	

1. The telemetry monitor detects the transition from armed: true to armed: false.

2. The backend verifies the current geographic coordinates match the intended drop-off zone.

3. The backend sends a PWM execution command to the designated auxiliary port on the flight controller.

4. The servo motor rotates 90 degrees, opening the mechanical latch.

5. The system waits 5 seconds to ensure payload clearance.

6. The drone re-arms and initiates the takeoff sequence to return home.
Alternative Flow	If the landing was triggered as an emergency failsafe outside the drop-off zone, the backend locks the payload controller and refuses to actuate the servo, returning the package safely to the home base.
3.5.2.7 Module 7: Mission Analytics & Logging Engine

This module generates business and diagnostic value from the delivery flights.

Table 3.8: UC-7.1: Mission Data Logging and Report Generation
Field	Description
Use Case ID	UC-7.1
Use Case Name	Mission Data Logging and Report Generation
Actors	System Administrator
Description	Captures high-frequency telemetry events and compiles them into a structured database for later administrative download and review.
Trigger	The drone successfully completes a mission and disarms at the Home pad.
Preconditions	The mission has concluded, and the drone's internal state manager has recorded flight metrics in RAM.
Postconditions	A persistent CSV log is generated and made available on the Admin Dashboard.
Normal Flow	

1. The mission sequence reaches the "Mission Complete" state.

2. The logging engine aggregates the timestamped telemetry data, battery consumption percentages, and Lidar evasion occurrence counts.

3. The data is written to a local SQLite database on the Raspberry Pi.

4. The System Administrator logs into the React.js Admin Panel.

5. The admin selects the respective Flight ID row and clicks "Download CSV".

6. The FastAPI backend streams the compiled log file to the user's browser.
Alternative Flow	If the drone crashes or powers off unexpectedly mid-flight, the system's persistent heartbeat logger will save a partial "Crash Report" up to the final millisecond of recorded telemetry for diagnostic analysis.
3.5.3 Event-Response Table

The event-response table defines how the Autonomous Delivery Drone reacts to significant internal processing thresholds and external environmental events throughout its operational lifecycle.

Table 3.9: Event-Response Table
Event	Source	System Response
User Login Attempt	Logistics Operator / Web UI	Validate credentials against the database; grant access to the GCS dashboard or deny and display error.
Dispatch Command Received	Logistics Operator	Verify GPS lock and battery; initialize MAVSDK offboard mode; arm motors; takeoff to 10m; begin velocity field navigation.
Destination Reached (Within 0.000015°)	GPS Telemetry Monitor	Halt continuous velocity field; command stable hover; trigger OpenCV Precision Touchdown protocol.
Obstacle Detected < 7.0m	ROS 2 Avoider Node (Lidar)	Broadcast DODGE vector; command drone to hover/level pitch; initiate blended lateral evasion slide.
Path "CLEAR" for 2 Seconds	ROS 2 Avoider Node (Lidar)	Clear DODGE memory; apply forward velocity to bypass edge; recalculate compass bearing; resume primary mission.
ArUco Marker Not Found (45s Timeout)	OpenCV Vision Node	Trigger Vision Failsafe; abort landing sequence; ascend to safe altitude; execute Return-to-Launch (RTL).
Motors Disarmed at Destination	Pixhawk Flight Controller	Trigger Actuation Controller; rotate PWM servo to release payload; wait 5 seconds; re-arm and return home.
Battery Critical Threshold Reached	Safety Monitor Daemon	Override all active tasks; if Level 1, trigger RTL. If Level 2, command immediate vertical emergency landing.
Telemetry Heartbeat Lost (>10s)	Safety Monitor Daemon	Trigger Network Failsafe; flight controller autonomously executes RTL based on last known home coordinates.

3.6.1 Module 1: Core Flight Control (FastAPI Backend)

3.6 Functional Requirements

Functional requirements define the core services and behaviors that the system shall provide. These requirements are derived directly from the use case model presented in Section 3.5. Each requirement is uniquely identified, measurable, and written using formal specification language. All functional requirements follow IEEE-style structure and are categorized by the system's seven primary architectural modules.
3.6.1 Module 1: Drone Flight Control (FastAPI Backend)
3.6.1.1 FR-1.1 Continuous Velocity Field Navigation

Table 3.10: Functional Requirement – Continuous Velocity Field Navigation
Identifier	FR-1.1
Title	Continuous Velocity Field Navigation
Requirement	The system shall utilize offboard VelocityNedYaw commands to continuously stream 10Hz movement vectors to the flight controller. It shall strictly avoid utilizing static GPS position-hold commands (e.g., goto_location) during the transit phase to ensure the Extended Kalman Filter (EKF) remains stable during dynamic evasion maneuvers.
Source	System Architecture Requirement
Rationale	Bypassing traditional waypoint navigation in favor of continuous velocity fields prevents sudden braking and maintains aerodynamic stability, enabling smooth trajectory blending when an obstacle is detected.
Dependencies	MAVSDK, PX4 Flight Controller
Priority	High
3.6.1.2 FR-1.2 Dynamic Compass Bearing Calculation

Table 3.11: Functional Requirement – Dynamic Bearing Calculation
Identifier	FR-1.2
Title	Dynamic Compass Bearing Calculation
Requirement	The system shall continuously and mathematically compute the geographic compass bearing (Yaw) between the drone's instantaneous telemetry coordinates and the final destination coordinates. It shall apply this calculated Yaw to all forward velocity vectors.
Source	Hardware Safety Requirement
Rationale	Ensures the drone's nose—and specifically the rigidly mounted 2D Lidar sensor—is perpetually facing the direction of travel, guaranteeing that upcoming physical obstructions are successfully scanned and detected.
Dependencies	GPS Telemetry, MAVSDK
Priority	High
3.6.2 Module 2: Web Ground Control Station (React.js Frontend)

Identifier	FR-2.1

Requirement	The system shall provide a React.js interactive map interface (utilizing Leaflet) that allows the Logistics Operator to drop a destination pin. The system shall automatically extract the precise latitudinal and longitudinal coordinates of this pin to generate the delivery mission payload.
Source	Stakeholder Requirement (Usability)

Identifier	FR-2.2

Source	Safety and Compliance Requirement
Rationale	Provides an essential human-in-the-loop failsafe to instantly halt operations and secure the airspace in the event of unexpected environmental hazards or hardware anomalies.
Dependencies	WebSocket Communication Bridge
Priority	High
3.6.3 Module 3: Spatial Reflex System (ROS 2 Obstacle Avoidance)
3.6.3.1 FR-3.1 Evasion Direction Locking

Table 3.14: Functional Requirement – Evasion Direction Locking
Identifier	FR-3.1
Title	Evasion Direction Locking and State Memory
Requirement	Upon detecting a solid obstacle breaching the 7.0-meter safety threshold, the ROS 2 node shall evaluate left and right proximity arrays, determine the optimal escape route, and lock this decision (e.g., DODGE_LEFT) into state memory. It shall continuously broadcast this locked vector until the obstacle is fully bypassed.
Source	System Architecture Requirement
Rationale	Prevents the "ping-pong effect," a highly unstable oscillation caused by the drone rapidly switching evasion directions due to noisy Lidar sensor returns scattered off the edges of physical walls.
Dependencies	ROS 2 Humble, ros_gz_bridge, 2D Lidar
Priority	High
3.6.3.2 FR-3.2 Hover and Verify Debounce

Table 3.15: Functional Requirement – Hover and Verify Debounce
Identifier	FR-3.2
Title	Hover and Verify Pitch-Blindness Debounce
Requirement	When an evasion command is intercepted, the FastAPI flight controller shall command an immediate aerodynamic deceleration (-2.5 m/s) followed by a 1.5-second stable hover. The system shall only process subsequent "CLEAR" signals from the ROS 2 node after this stabilization period.
Source	Robotics Physics Constraint
Rationale	Directly resolves "Lidar Pitch-Blindness." Hard braking causes the drone to tilt backward, pointing the 2D Lidar at the sky. A mandatory hover levels the drone, ensuring the Lidar is scanning the actual environment before lateral sliding commences.
Dependencies	FR-1.1, FR-3.1
Priority	High
3.6.4 Module 4: Precision Vision System (OpenCV)
3.6.4.1 FR-4.1 Visual Error Calculation

Table 3.16: Functional Requirement – Visual Error Calculation
Identifier	FR-4.1
Title	ArUco Marker Identification and Visual Error Calculation
Requirement	The system shall continuously process downward-facing camera frames at high frequencies to detect predefined ArUco marker dictionaries. Upon detection, the system shall calculate the precise X and Y pixel deviation of the marker's center relative to the camera frame's absolute center.
Source	Core Processing Requirement
Rationale	Standard GPS modules suffer from 2-3 meters of geographic drift. Computer vision provides the millimeter-accurate alignment required to land safely on a confined delivery pad.
Dependencies	OpenCV, Downward Camera
Priority	High
3.6.4.2 FR-4.2 Proportional Descent Control

Table 3.17: Functional Requirement – Proportional Descent Control
Identifier	FR-4.2
Title	Proportional Descent Velocity Control
Requirement	The flight control backend shall receive the OpenCV pixel error over UDP and apply a calibrated Proportional gain (Kp​=0.015). It shall translate these mathematical errors into physical lateral velocity adjustments while maintaining a continuous vertical descent of 0.4 m/s.
Source	Robotics Control Logic Requirement
Rationale	Ensures the drone actively fights environmental wind drift and micro-corrects its trajectory dynamically during the landing sequence, preventing pendulum oscillations.
Dependencies	FR-4.1, MAVSDK
Priority	High

Source	Safety Standard Requirement

Source	Business Logic / Mechanical Requirement
Rationale	Prevents premature mid-air package drops that could damage the payload or the drone's aerodynamic stability, ensuring the package is only released when safely grounded.

3.7 Non-Functional Requirements

Non-functional requirements define quality attributes that describe how the system performs rather than specific behaviors. These requirements are measurable and support system reliability, usability, performance, security, and maintainability. For a cyber-physical system like an autonomous UAV, these attributes are critical to ensuring safe and stable flight operations.
3.7.1 Performance Requirements

    PER-1: The ROS 2 Lidar spatial reflex node shall process 360-degree proximity arrays and compute evasion velocity vectors at a minimum frequency of 10Hz. This ensures the drone has sufficient reaction time to smoothly bypass obstacles detected at the 7.0-meter safety threshold while traveling at cruising speeds.

    PER-2: The OpenCV Precision Vision System shall process downward-facing camera frames with a maximum latency of 100 milliseconds per frame. High-speed visual error calculation is required to prevent over-correction and dangerous pendulum oscillations during the drone's descent phase.

    PER-3: The FastAPI communication bridge shall transmit live telemetry data (Altitude, Battery, GPS, Mode, Lidar State) via WebSockets to the React.js frontend at a minimum update rate of 5Hz to provide the operator with real-time situational awareness.

    PER-4: The React.js Ground Control Station dashboard, including the interactive Leaflet map and UI components, shall load and become fully interactive within 3 seconds under normal network conditions.

3.7.2 Security Requirements

    SEC-1: Internal microservice communication ports (UDP Port 5005 for Vision, UDP Port 5006 for Lidar) shall be strictly bound to the local loopback address (127.0.0.1) on the companion computer. This prevents malicious external actors from spoofing UDP packets to hijack the drone's spatial reflex or landing commands.

    SEC-2: The FastAPI backend shall implement secure authentication mechanisms for its REST API endpoints, ensuring that only authorized logistics operators can issue the Dispatch or Takeoff commands to the flight controller.

    SEC-3: All external MAVLink and WebSocket communications between the Ground Control Station (frontend laptop/tablet) and the drone's onboard Raspberry Pi shall be transmitted over a securely encrypted WPA2/WPA3 local Wi-Fi network.

    SEC-4: The system shall enforce role-based access control, ensuring that standard operators can only dispatch and monitor flights, while only System Administrators can access and download internal mission logs and CSV analytics.

3.7.3 Reliability Requirements

    REL-1: The system's flight architecture shall maintain Extended Kalman Filter (EKF) stability by strictly utilizing continuous VelocityNedYaw commands. It shall prohibit sudden raw position holds (goto_location) or extreme reverse velocity injections during high-speed transits to prevent aerodynamic stalling and mid-air crashes.

    REL-2: The Safety Monitor daemon shall implement an automated battery failsafe. If battery voltage drops below 15%, the system shall override all active autonomous scripts and execute a Return-to-Launch (RTL). If voltage drops below 5%, it shall command an immediate vertical emergency landing.

    REL-3: The system shall implement a network heartbeat monitor. If the telemetry connection between the Ground Control Station and the drone is severed for more than 10 continuous seconds, the drone shall autonomously trigger a hardware-level RTL to prevent the UAV from flying blindly beyond recovery range.

    REL-4: The Precision Vision System shall utilize a 45-second timeout mechanism. If the drone reaches the destination but cannot identify the ArUco marker within 45 seconds (due to occlusion or lighting loss), the system shall safely abort the landing and return home.

3.7.4 Usability Requirements

    USE-1: The React.js interface shall significantly reduce operator cognitive load by completely abstracting complex technical engineering parameters (such as PID tuning variables, raw MAVLink parameters, and yaw rates) from the primary dispatch screen.

    USE-2: The system shall follow standard UI/UX design principles by providing clear, color-coded visual indicators on the map: a highly visible drone icon for live tracking, a red pin for the drop-off destination, and a dotted line representing the calculated trajectory.

    USE-3: Critical safety interventions shall be highly accessible. The user interface shall feature a prominent, zero-latency "EMERGENCY LAND" button that is permanently visible during all active flight modes.

    USE-4: The Ground Control Station application shall be responsive and cross-platform, allowing dispatchers to accurately plan missions and monitor telemetry seamlessly across laptop web browsers and standard-sized tablet devices.

3.7.5 Scalability Requirements

    SCA-1: The system shall follow a highly decoupled, microservice-based architecture. The ROS 2 Lidar node, OpenCV Vision node, and FastAPI flight controller must operate as independent processes, allowing the system to scale hardware components (e.g., upgrading from a 2D RPLidar to a 3D Depth Camera) without requiring a rewrite of the core flight logic.

    SCA-2: The local SQLite database designed for mission analytics and logging shall efficiently support the storage of high-frequency telemetry events across hundreds of simulated and physical flights without degrading backend read/write performance.

    SCA-3: The React.js frontend architecture shall be designed to allow future horizontal scaling, laying the groundwork for fleet management where a single operator dashboard could simultaneously track multiple autonomous delivery drones on the same map interface.

3.7.6 Maintainability Requirements

    MAIN-1: The system shall adhere strictly to modular architecture principles. Vision, spatial reflexes, user interface, and MAVLink telemetry translation must remain isolated in their respective directories and technology stacks.

    MAIN-2: All Python, ROS 2, and React source code shall be thoroughly documented. Complex geospatial mathematics, including dynamic geographic compass bearing functions and offset waypoint rotation matrices, must include in-line mathematical explanations to facilitate future academic handovers.

    MAIN-3: The software stack shall utilize standardized, actively supported frameworks (ROS 2 Humble, MAVSDK-Python, standard Gazebo Harmonic plugins) to ensure that future firmware updates from the PX4 Autopilot team can be integrated without necessitating major system restructuring.

3.8 External Interface Requirements

This section defines how the Autonomous Delivery Drone system interacts with external entities, including human logistics operators, onboard hardware devices, integrated software frameworks, and local communication networks. Clear interface definitions are critical for maintaining the stability of the decoupled microservice architecture.
3.8.1 User Interface Requirements

    UI-1: The system shall provide a graphical Ground Control Station (GCS) interface accessible via standard modern web browsers (built using React.js).

    UI-2: The interface shall integrate a high-resolution, interactive map component (via Leaflet) that allows users to place destination pins and visualize the drone's live trajectory.

    UI-3: The system shall utilize standardized, high-contrast color coding for critical operational controls (e.g., Green for "Confirm Mission & Dispatch", Red for "Emergency Land") to reduce operator error.

    UI-4: The layout shall be responsive across devices (laptops and tablets) to accommodate on-field logistics operators.

    UI-5: The dashboard sidebar shall dynamically render live telemetry data (Altitude, Battery, Mode, Lidar State) without requiring manual page reloads.

3.8.2 Hardware Interface Requirements

    HI-1: The high-level Python flight logic, ROS 2 middleware, and OpenCV processes shall operate concurrently on a standard onboard companion computer (e.g., Raspberry Pi 4 or 5).

    HI-2: The companion computer shall interface physically with a MAVLink-compatible flight controller (e.g., Pixhawk) via a USB or UART serial connection.

    HI-3: The system shall interface with a 2D Lidar sensor (e.g., RPLidar A1) via a high-speed serial port to ingest 360-degree environmental proximity arrays.

    HI-4: The system shall interface with a downward-facing USB webcam to capture real-time image frames for the ArUco marker detection pipeline.

    HI-5: The flight controller shall interface with a PWM (Pulse Width Modulation) servo motor connected to a designated auxiliary output channel to mechanically actuate the payload release latch.

3.8.3 Software Interface Requirements

    SI-1: The FastAPI flight controller backend shall interface directly with the PX4 Autopilot firmware utilizing the asynchronous MAVSDK-Python library.

    SI-2: The obstacle avoidance module shall interface with the ROS 2 Humble middleware environment, utilizing the ros_gz_bridge to subscribe to standard /lidar/scan sensor messages.

    SI-3: The Precision Vision System shall interface with the OpenCV (cv2) library to access pre-trained ArUco dictionaries and perform coordinate matrix calculations.

    SI-4: The Mission Analytics module shall interface with a local SQLite (or equivalent relational database) engine to reliably write flight logs and query historical evasion events.

3.8.4 Communication Interface Requirements

    CI-1: The React.js frontend and FastAPI backend shall execute bi-directional, real-time communication utilizing the WebSocket protocol over a secure local Wi-Fi network.

    CI-2: The high-level Python backend shall communicate telemetry and movement vectors to the Pixhawk flight controller using the MAVLink protocol over UDP (e.g., udp://:14540).

    CI-3: The decoupled internal microservices (ROS 2 Lidar Node and OpenCV Vision Node) shall broadcast their dynamic evasion and alignment vectors to the central flight controller via strictly bound local UDP sockets (127.0.0.1:5005 and 127.0.0.1:5006).

    CI-4: Standard HTTP RESTful API endpoints shall be used for non-continuous backend communications, such as submitting the initial dispatch coordinates and downloading generated CSV flight reports.

3.9 Assumptions and Dependencies

This section lists the critical assumptions and system dependencies established during the requirement analysis and system design planning phases. These constraints dictate the operational boundaries of the cyber-physical system.

Assumptions:

    Network Connectivity: The system assumes a stable, low-latency local Wi-Fi connection (2.4GHz/5GHz) between the Ground Control Station (frontend) and the drone's companion computer to maintain WebSocket telemetry and REST API dispatch commands.

    Operational Environment: The system assumes operations remain strictly within Visual Line of Sight (VLOS) and occur under adequate daylight or well-lit conditions, as the OpenCV precision landing pipeline cannot function in absolute darkness.

    Hardware Calibration: The drone's physical frame, Center of Gravity (CoG), and electronic speed controllers (ESCs) are assumed to be properly calibrated, ensuring aerodynamic stability during aggressive lateral evasion maneuvers.

    Firmware Failsafe Configuration: It is assumed that internal PX4 Autopilot parameters (such as COM_LOW_BAT_ACT = -1) are correctly configured by the operator prior to flight to prevent built-in hardware failsafes from prematurely hijacking the Python execution loops.

Dependencies:

    Middleware Dependency: The spatial reflex system is strictly dependent on the availability and stability of the ROS 2 Humble framework and the ros_gz_bridge for sensor data translation.

    Flight Stack Dependency: Core flight control logic relies heavily on the MAVSDK-Python library to successfully translate high-level Python functions into low-level MAVLink protocols.

    Computer Vision Dependency: The precision touchdown sequence is highly dependent on the OpenCV (cv2) library and access to predefined ArUco marker dictionaries.

3.4 System Overview

The proposed Autonomous Delivery Drone operates within a highly structured, hybrid edge-computing environment designed to support modular interaction between the web-based user interfaces, high-level processing logic, and low-level aerodynamic hardware. The system supports role-based human interactions and ensures secure, real-time communication between internal microservices (ROS 2, OpenCV, FastAPI) and the external flight controller via MAVLink, UDP, and WebSocket protocols.

Table 3.1: User Classes and Characteristics
User Class	Description	Technical Level
Logistics Operator	Uses the React.js Ground Control Station (GCS) to perform core functionalities such as map-based destination selection, drone dispatch, and live telemetry monitoring.	Basic
System Administrator	Manages system configurations, accesses internal SQLite databases, reviews post-mission Lidar evasion logs, and downloads CSV flight analytics.	Advanced
Automated Subsystems (ROS 2 / OpenCV)	Internal autonomous actors providing domain-specific services, including 2D spatial reflexes (obstacle avoidance) and visual pixel-error calculation for landing.	Technical
PX4 Flight Controller (External System)	The physical flight management unit interacting with the FastAPI backend via the MAVLink API to translate velocity vectors into raw motor mixing.	Technical

The overall architecture follows a decoupled microservices and edge-computing pipeline specifically designed for robotics. The system boundary includes all internal software modules running on the onboard companion computer (FastAPI flight logic, ROS 2 Avoider node, OpenCV vision node), the remote React.js user interface, local data logging components, and the integrated MAVSDK libraries.

By segregating the architecture into independent nodes communicating over local UDP sockets, the system ensures that computationally heavy tasks—such as processing high-frequency Lidar proximity arrays and generating bounding boxes for ArUco markers—do not block or delay the critical, real-time safety loops of the main flight controller.

5. Describe your idea briefly. (within 250 words)*
Our startup idea centers on developing an Autonomous Last-Mile Delivery Drone System tailored for local logistics. The system comprises a physical quadcopter (UAV) integrated with a highly intuitive, React.js-based Web Ground Control Station (GCS).

Unlike standard radio-controlled drones, our product completely removes the need for a trained pilot. A dispatcher simply drops a pin on a web map and clicks "Dispatch." The drone's onboard edge-computer handles the rest using a sophisticated microservice architecture. It utilizes Continuous Velocity Field Navigation to travel to the destination, a dedicated ROS 2 (Robot Operating System) Lidar node to actively detect and dodge unmapped urban obstacles in real-time, and an OpenCV computer vision pipeline to recognize printed ArUco landing pads for millimeter-accurate, wind-corrected payload drops.

By abstracting complex robotics into a simple web dashboard, we aim to provide a cost-effective, traffic-independent aerial logistics fleet for local businesses, pharmacies, and couriers.

6. Describe your product and the problem you are trying to solve. (within 400 words)*
The Problem:
In the modern supply chain, "last-mile delivery"—the final step of getting a product to the consumer's doorstep—is notoriously the most expensive and inefficient phase. It is plagued by heavy urban traffic congestion, high fuel costs, and reliance on human labor. While multi-billion-dollar corporations like Amazon and Alphabet (Wing) are solving this using proprietary drone networks, these closed-ecosystem solutions are entirely inaccessible to local courier services, pharmacies, and small e-commerce businesses in developing regions. Furthermore, existing open-source drone software (like QGroundControl) is designed for aerospace engineers, requiring immense technical training that standard delivery dispatchers do not possess.

The Product:
We are building a decentralized, accessible Autonomous Aerial Delivery System. Our product bridges the gap between high-level web technologies and low-level reactive flight dynamics.

It consists of two main components:

    The Web GCS (Ground Control Station): A streamlined web dashboard that allows a logistics dispatcher to manage deliveries just like a ride-sharing app. It requires zero technical flight knowledge.

    The Smart UAV: A quadcopter equipped with a companion computer (Raspberry Pi), a 2D Lidar sensor, and a downward-facing camera.

Our drone survives unpredictable urban environments autonomously. If a physical obstruction blocks its path, our proprietary ROS 2 Spatial Reflex node calculates a dynamic evasion vector, seamlessly curving the drone around the obstacle without stopping. Upon reaching the destination, it does not rely on inaccurate GPS to land; instead, it uses a computer vision camera to lock onto a printed landing pad, calculating pixel-errors to execute a perfect payload drop.

By providing an open-architecture, localized fleet solution, we are empowering local businesses to bypass traffic, reduce delivery times from hours to minutes, and cut human labor costs for lightweight, urgent deliveries.

11. What is different, interesting, and innovative about your product?*
There are two major innovations in our product:

    Continuous Velocity Blending for Evasion: Standard drones use static GPS waypoints; if a building is in the way, they stop and often crash due to "Lidar pitch-blindness." Our drone utilizes "Potential Field Navigation." It never stops to plan; it constantly blends forward velocity with lateral Lidar evasion vectors, smoothly curving around obstacles like a fish swimming around a rock, maintaining perfect aerodynamic stability.

    Zero-Training Operations: We have completely abstracted the robotics away from the user. We replaced complex engineering dashboards with a modern, React.js web app. A delivery dispatcher can route a drone exactly as easily as they would order an Uber, bringing enterprise-grade aerial logistics to standard retail employees.

12. Who may be your competitors?*

    Direct Aerial Competitors: Wing (Alphabet), Amazon Prime Air, Zipline, and Manna Drone Delivery (though these are proprietary and largely operate in Western markets).

    Indirect/Traditional Competitors: Local road-based courier networks (TCS, Leopard), food delivery services (Foodpanda), and standard human-driven motorcycle delivery fleets.

13. What is your target market?*
Our initial target market consists of B2B (Business-to-Business) local enterprises that require rapid, traffic-independent transport of lightweight, high-value, or urgent goods (under 500 grams). This includes:

    Pharmacies & Hospitals: For emergency medicine, blood, or lab sample transfers.

    E-commerce & Local Retailers: For premium "under-30-minutes" local delivery services.

    Local Courier Hubs: To handle the immediate last-mile leg of local document/parcel drops.

14. Would you be able to prepare the prototype and validate the market within six months?*
Yes. We have already completed the most difficult phase: the architectural software engineering. Our hybrid stack (FastAPI, ROS 2, React.js, and OpenCV) has been successfully programmed and rigorously stress-tested in the Gazebo Harmonic 3D physics simulator.
With SSBC funding, the next six months will be strictly dedicated to procuring the physical hardware (Pixhawk, Lidar, Companion Computer, Quadcopter frame), assembling the physical prototype, conducting real-world field trials, and demonstrating a live delivery payload drop to local business stakeholders for market validation.

Tell the committee: "We aren't just building a drone; we are building a software platform that lets any local pharmacy run its own automated delivery fleet." Best of luck! 5. Describe your idea briefly. (within 250 words)*
Our startup provides an enterprise-grade Autonomous Last-Mile Delivery Drone System designed specifically for local B2B logistics. We are transforming how local businesses—such as pharmacies, pathological labs, and e-commerce vendors—handle urgent, lightweight deliveries.

Instead of just building a drone, we have engineered a complete, software-first aerial logistics platform. The core of our product is a highly intuitive, React.js-based Web Ground Control Station. A dispatcher with zero pilot training simply selects a map destination and clicks "Dispatch."

Our proprietary edge-computing architecture handles the flight entirely autonomously. Using Continuous Velocity Field Navigation, ROS 2-powered Lidar obstacle avoidance, and OpenCV-based precision landing, our drones dynamically survive unpredictable urban environments without human intervention. By abstracting complex robotics into a simple web dashboard, we offer a decentralized, traffic-independent delivery fleet that slashes last-mile delivery times from hours to minutes, significantly reducing operational costs for local enterprises.

6. Describe your product and the problem you are trying to solve. (within 400 words)*
The Problem:
Last-mile delivery is the most expensive, inefficient, and time-consuming segment of the global supply chain, often accounting for over 50% of total shipping costs. In urban environments, traditional courier methods rely on human-driven motorcycles or vans, which are severely bottlenecked by traffic congestion, rising fuel costs, and high labor turnover. While tech giants like Amazon and Alphabet operate proprietary delivery drones, these closed ecosystems are entirely inaccessible to local businesses in developing markets. Furthermore, existing open-source drone software is built for aerospace engineers, presenting an insurmountable learning curve for standard logistics dispatchers.

The Product (Our Solution):
We offer a decentralized "Drone-as-a-Service" software and hardware platform. Our product enables any local business to operate its own automated delivery fleet without hiring specialized drone pilots.

How it works:
The user logs into our Web Dashboard, drops a pin on the delivery location, and dispatches the UAV. Our onboard microservice architecture (combining FastAPI, ROS 2, and OpenCV) takes full control. The drone uses a 2D Lidar sensor to actively detect and smoothly bypass unmapped obstacles in real-time, completely avoiding the fatal "stop-and-crash" flaws of standard GPS drones. Upon arrival, it does not rely on inaccurate GPS drifting; instead, it uses computer vision to detect a printed landing pad, calculating pixel-errors to execute a wind-corrected, mathematically precise touchdown.

Business Impact:
We eliminate traffic delays, drastically reduce fuel and labor costs, and provide a secure, low-latency delivery channel for high-value goods (such as emergency medical supplies or priority documents). By turning complex robotics into a simple, web-based dispatch system, we democratize aerial logistics for local economies, enabling faster, cheaper, and safer deliveries.

11. What is different, interesting, and innovative about your product?*
Our product holds three distinct competitive advantages:

    Zero-Training Dispatch: We replaced complex engineering ground stations with a consumer-friendly web app. If a dispatcher can order an Uber, they can operate our drone fleet.

    Continuous Velocity Evasion: Standard drones fly blindly point-to-point. Our ROS 2 Lidar integration allows the drone to dynamically "slide" around unexpected obstacles in real-time without stopping, maintaining critical aerodynamic stability.

    Cost-Effective Precision: Instead of relying on expensive RTK-GPS systems ($1000+) for accurate landing, we engineered an OpenCV (Computer Vision) pipeline that uses a standard, low-cost camera to lock onto a printed pad, executing millimeter-accurate landings. This drastically lowers our hardware capital expenditure, making the business highly scalable.

12. Who may be your competitors?*

    Direct Global Competitors: Wing, Zipline, and Manna Drone Delivery. (These validate the massive global demand for this technology, but they do not serve our local markets).

    Indirect/Local Competitors: Traditional courier services (TCS, Leopards), ride-hailing delivery services (Bykea, InDrive), and in-house delivery riders.

    Our Edge: We are significantly faster than road-based couriers and vastly cheaper to deploy than enterprise drone networks.

13. What is your target market?*
Our initial Go-To-Market (GTM) strategy targets B2B local enterprises handling lightweight, time-sensitive, and high-margin goods.

    Healthcare & Pharma: Urgent transport of critical medicines, blood bags, and lab samples between hospitals and diagnostic centers.

    Local Quick-Commerce: Premium, under-30-minute delivery for high-value electronics, spare parts, or legal documents.

    Courier Hubs: Assisting existing courier services with bypassing local traffic bottlenecks for "last-mile" neighborhood drops.

14. Would you be able to prepare the prototype and validate the market within six months?*
Yes. The highest-risk phase—the architectural software engineering—is already complete. Our hybrid software stack (ROS 2, React.js, FastAPI, OpenCV) has been successfully programmed and rigorously validated in the Gazebo 3D physics simulator.

With SSBC funding, our 6-month execution roadmap is:

    Months 1-2 (Hardware Integration): Procure physical components (Pixhawk, Lidar, Companion PC, Frame) and flash our existing software stack onto the physical hardware.

    Months 3-4 (Field Trials): Conduct controlled Visual Line of Sight (VLOS) testing to tune aerodynamic PID loops, Lidar reflex latency, and OpenCV landing accuracy in real-world wind conditions.

    Months 5-6 (Market Validation): Partner with a local pharmacy or campus department to conduct a live, documented pilot delivery program. This pilot will prove our operational efficiency and help us secure our first Letter of Intent (LOI) from a B2B client.
